LONDON, Miy 21.â AÂ» dispatch from
Middelburg, Cape Colony, says an Impor
tant concentration of Boers is proceeding
in Zuurberg. Many fresh invaders are
crossing the Orange River into Cape Col
ony, and Commandant Fouche also Joined
the burghers in that district. The total
strength of the Boers in that . district is
estimated at between 10,000 and 15.000.
There is an unconfirmed rumor that Gen
eral Christian Dewet is again in command
of the Boer invaders. British military of
ficials are cognizant of the movements of
the Boers, and are prepared for them.

do anything in the matter. â¢, ' v :

when he rejoined McClellan, /moving

This was on account of charges preferred
by General Pope. of. misconduct and cow
ardice .during Stonewall Jackson's flank
movement â¢ in ; the summer of 1862 around
Pope's army and the fighting leading up
to the second battle of Manassas.
â¢; A court-martiar convicted General Por
ter and he was cashiered from the army.
Not till' sixteen years after, in 1878, when
the Comte de Paris, in his "History 'of
the War of the Rebellion," Had demon
strated that Porter was not In fault, and
Porter and his friends had made repeated
efforts to that end, was a court of inquiry
appointed to examine into the findings of
the court-martial and. report thereon.
This court, consisting of Generals Scho
fleld, Terry and Getty, completely vindi
cated the much-wronged Porter. General
Grant approved the finding and exerted
himself to have Porter reinstated in his'
rank in the army. A bill removing his
disabilities was passed after a fierce fight
in both houses of Congress, but was ve
toed by President Arthur. Another bill,
practically the same, was passed In 1886
and became a law. - He was restored to
his rank, but no provision was made for
his back -pay. He not long after retired,
after which he held the positions of Com
missioner of Public Works in New York
City, assistant receiver of the New Jersey
Central Railroad for several years and
then Police Commissioner of New York
City. ; On the expiration of his term In
the latter office he was appointed Fire
Commissioner, from which place he re
tired at the end of his term and did not
after "hold any public office. Â¦ In all his
military career General 1 Porter showed
himself to be a thoroughly capable, judi
cious, brave and successful soldier.

Great Benefits, of Education.
The- President" stood- up in his carriage

I ' desire to - express the pleasure * which'
has been elven I to me to meet i with' the
45,000 school 'children . of Â¦ the 'â¢-. city of -'San
Francisco. It has given. me -an Introduction
Into the- countleES'homes of your. great city and
has permitted me to witnenB the sunshine -which'
this vast number of young people bring to the
firesides of the city.. I know of no richer pos
session than great scholarship, no nobler ambi
tion than to attain It. We cannot all be great
scholars.. but we can all have good scholarship.
Your State constitution early provided for a
greats public school-system', and- declared that
the diffusion of. knowledge and Intelligence was
Indispensable to. the Independence and liberties
of the people. You have 260,000 school children
in the public schools of the State of California,
and your State annually expends for your en
lightenment and equipment to â¢ perform â¢ the
great duties of life more than Blx millions of
dollars, and I want to assure you young people
that there is nothing so essential to your easy
advancement and success In after life as a good
education. .1 If those of us who have battled in
the rivalries and contentions of a busy world
could go back to our youth and our school days
we would embrace cheerfully our neglected op
portunities and pursue- them with industry and
delight. If the testimony of the active men of
San Francisco and of the country could be
taken it would be uniform In the declaration
of thÂ« embarrassments under which they -have
suffered from scanty mental training in youth.
Praises Our Schools and Universities.

everywhere throughout trie State, and then you
have the two great universitiesâ the Stanford

Fitz John Porter was born at~ Ports
mouth, N. H., In 1822. He was a nephew
of the famous Commodore Porter, who
commanded the American ship Essex dur
ing the war o J- 1812-14. Â¦ He graduated from
West Point in 1845. and then entered upon
a career of usefulness' and gallantry
which give him a line reputation. .In 1846
he went to the Mexican war and took
part in all the fighting from Vera Cruz
to Mexico, being brevetted . for gallant
and meritorious conduct at Molino del
Rey and the City of -Mexico. From 1849
to 1856 he was on duty at .West Point. In
1856 he was attached to the staff of Gen
eral PerBlfer Smith, commanding in Kan
sas during the troubles in that State, and
in 1857 went with General Albert Sidney
Johnson on the Utah expedition. On the
secession of Texas In 1861 -he was sent to
withdraw the United States troops from
the posts in that State, which he accom
plished with success and much credit. He
organized the three months 1 ' volunteers
from -Pennsylvania which made the nu
cleus of the . Army., of the Potomac, ,He
was then , made cblonel of the Fifteenth

their grievances last night at 1133 Mission

NO more* orderly body of men
seeking an adjudication of dif
ferences between themselves and
their employers could be found
than the ironworkers now out
on Etrike. They met at their headquar
ters at 323 Sixth street yesterday from
early morning till late at night. Condi
tions arising out of each shop were dis
cussed and while many of them talked
to others In their endeavor to make a
hasty settlement, no results were reached.
One feature of the strike that is of the
utmost importance is the action to be
taken by the Iron Molders. Those men
have been through a strike before and
while their sympathies are with their
brothers in the trade they may hesitate
to take definite action.
At the Potrero, where are located the
Union and Risdon Iron Works, the lead
ing shipbuilding plants on the Western
Coast, the sound of hammers was not
heard. At the Fulton Jron Works, in
the northern end of the peninsula, the
came quiet reigned. At noon time some
thirty or forty iron molders walked out,
leaving the work practically in the hands
of the apprentice bcyB. . "
At the Union Iron Works a paying tell
er disbursed the wage* coming to the
men who left on Monday morning. The
same plan of payment will be pursued
until each man has received his due. It
is said that the works, formerly employ
ing nearly 40W men, will be closed down
for an indefinite period until the differ
ences between employers and employes
are adjusted.

Firms Sign "Agreements.
No regularly declared strike of the iron
molders will : be inaugurated to-day. At
a meeting held last night the question as
to whether a strike should be declared in
sympathy with the machinists was ex
haustively discussed, but no definite 'ac
tion was taken. The meeting was finally
adjourned until to-night, when a larger
hall will be secured and the question tak
en up again. As yet no communication
has been received from the national body
of iron workers and without their sanc
tion the local body is prohibited from
striking. The iron molders who have
tjius far left their work have done so
on their own responsibility.
At the. meeting last night many vehe
ment speeches were delivered In favor of
an immediate strike, but on the other
hand the more conservative element ad
vised caution. A general feeling prevails
among the iron workers that the meeting
to-night will result in a general strike. It
is expected that the national body, will
declare its position to-day.
Those who are looking after the general
interests of labor are casting about, for
some disinterested individual to act as
arbitrator. Some names have been sug
gested and are now -under consideration.
Bishop William Ford Nichols of the Epis
copal church, Archbishop Hiordan of the
Catholic church and Bishop John W.
Hamilton of the Methodist Episcopal
church have been suggested as probable
mediators. In the scope of the plan out
lined by -the Building Trades Council it
is proposed to peacefully settle all differ
ences by arbitration. But as. there are so
many different features involved in the
of the problem now confronting
the labor movement it is very "difficult to
find one who will attempt to emulate the
example given by Bishop Potter of New
York.

"WASHINGTON. May 21.â The case of
the Business Men's League of St.- Louis
against the Southern Pacific and other
transcontinental line*, involving Issues of
great importance' to shippers to Pacific
Coast points was heard by the Interstate
Commerce Commission to-day. The case
has been pending in various ways for a
long time, and after a hearing: that closed
late this afternoon tne commission fixed
October 22, In this city, for the hearing of
arguments In the case. /
St. Louis and Chicago Shippers are try
ing to secure lower rates to the Pacific
Coast from the Middle West and to have
the roads reduce the difference between
carload rates and leas than carload rates
in the Pacific Coast business. Vice Presi
dent Aftrton and Traffic* Manager Biddte
represented the Santa Fe; Chief Council
W. O. Herrin and Vice President Stubbx,
the Southern Pacific; H. L. Christie, F.
M. Johnson of St. Louis and J. F. Tomp
klns of Chicago represented the Business
Men's League, and E. S. Plllsbury the
Pacific Coast Jobbers.
Vice President Morton was the principal
witness to-day. While he knew many rail
road officials differed with his views, he
believed that the difference between the
carload and less than carload rates should
be decreased. The difference, he said, wa3
too great from the standpoint of both the
carrier and the small shipper.. He said
it did not cost the railroads to handle the
two quantities of goods an amount com
mensurate with the difference in rates. Ha
believed the difference should be basd on
the cost of handling. It was right that
this difference should be decreased, al
though he said it eight be Impracticable
to bring It about. Custom had brought
the present difference, and the action of
any individual road could not break the
practice. , .- - * -
Morton also favored graded rates on
transcontinental business as a fair and
equitable measure for both the carriers
and the shippers.' Vice President Stubba
of the Southern Pacific testified in con
travention of the position taken by Mr.
Morton.

DOWIE IS A MONEY-MAKER
Exposure of His Methods Here
Caused Him to Go to Chicago.
John Alexander Dowie, general overseer
of the "Christian Catholic church," to
whose frauds and pretentious impostures
the press and the law in Chicago seem at
last to be awakening, is not unknown to
the readers of The Call, in whose columns
full exposition of his methods and doings
was made when he attempted "work" in
this vicinity.
"Doctor" Dowie made his appearance in
Oakland inlS94, and by a piece of bold
Imposition In the absence of the pastor se
cured from the trustees the use of the
First Presbyterian Church for his meet
ings and thereby gained a foothold In the
community. His true character and pre
tensions being exposed,, however, he was
driven from that building, but continued
to hold his meetings elsewhere for sev
eral months. He claimed to have "divine
powers" of healing, and announced that
ho had come- to Oakland to start an
"Eden," that the blind should see, the
deaf hear and the lame, warm themselves
by the fire made of their useless crutches.
He opened an office adjoining his meet
ing place, and his private practice grew
to enormous proportions. His charges
were from $1 to $10, and he banked large
sums every, day. He claimed to have
healed a blind girl and to have caused

Fault McGrane has been in the habit of
beating his wife. On Friday night screams
were heard coming from the McGrane res
idence. Those who looked into the open
doer sa-w Mrs. McGrane running about the
room Fcr*=aming for mercy. She was be
ing laFhp<i over the head! and about the

was ordered to desist and reluctantly
obeyed.
McGrane stated that he had tried to cor
rect his children, who had stayed away
from pchool. His wife interfered and the
assault followed. Mrs. McGrane Is well
spoken of by her neighbors.

STOCKTOX. May 2Lâ Tbe Grand Lodge
of the Knights of Pythias was formally
convened this forenoon in Masonic Music
Hall. The work of conferring the Grand
Lodge rank on past chancellors was con
tinued from last evening. About 200 will
receive the rank during this session. The
reports of the various officers were read
and sent to the proper committees. A
telegram was directed to President Mc-
Klnley In San Francisco asking him to
visit the Grand Lodge. To Supreme Chan
cellor Ogdcn H. Fethers, at Janesville.
Â¦\Vis.. was sent a message regretting his
inability to be here, he having been called
home after starting for Stockton.
The executive committee cf the Grand
Lodge met this evening to plan for the
Supreme Grand Lodge meeting in San
Francisco in 1902. The subordinate lodges
have been paying a small tax toward de
fraying the expense of the supreme gath
ering and have now $7500 in bank In San
Francisco. There is about $20,000 to be
raised yet by a continuance of the tax.
There has accumulated in the Pythian
home fund J9500. A site has been offered
at Woodbridge. In this county, and the
committee of the Grand Lodge will visit
it this week.
Aspirants for IiOdge Honors.
It is now about certain that there will
be no contest for the highest two offices.
Vice Chancellor Calkins will beco.me
chancellor and Grand Prelate Trask will
become vice chancellor. The grand
prelate's office leing considered the step
ping stone to the principal office, there
will perhaps be a hard contest for that.
Those who are dn the fight already are
D. C. Clark of Santa Cruz, B. V. Sargent
of Salinas and W. J. Tuska of San Fran
cisco. There no doubt will be others. For
grand master-at-arms and grand inner
guard there will be a lively scramble.

ir>et simultaneously to-day with the Grand

sentation speech was made by Mrs. Lydla
A. Monroe, supreme junior, in behalf of
Dunston Temple Xo. 3 of San Diego,
which was Instituted on May 8 by Mrs.
Stlbber.s. It was made of orange wood
and appropriately inscribed, on one side
being a gold monogram of the grand
chiefs initials.
Two other gravels were presented to the
Grar.d Temple for its permanent use. the
speech being made by Mrs. Sallie Wolf,
past grand chief of Sacramento. They
were from Mrs. Eva Simpson, grand mis
tress of finance, and her husband. They
were made from the wood of a. curly ma
ple grown In the yard of a knight at
Santa Rosa.

TINDER WEIGHT OF SNOW
Mineral King, a Summer Betreat
: Near Visalia, Reported to Be
in Ruins.
. VISALIA. May 21.â Word reached this
city this afternoon that Mineral King, a
village sixty-five miles distant in ] the
mountains, was in ruins. The damage was
caused by the heavy snowfall, the weight
of the snow causing two-thirds of the
houses to collapse. Much valuable prop
erty belonging to Visalla people Is affect
ed. The elegant summer home of John O.
Thomas of this city is among the wrecked
houses. At daylight to-morrow men will
be dispatched to the scene.

War Veteran Kills Himself During a
PORTLAND, Ore., May 2Lâ General
William Kapus killed himself with a re
volver this morning during a fit* of ' tem
porary insanity. He was 66 years of age.
He -was prominent in. the affair of Ore
gon and "Washington Territory for many
years. In 1890 General Kapus was United
States Consul at Sydney, Australia. Dur
ing the Civil War he 'was mustering and
disbursing officer for Oregon and Wash
ington! and served in Montana . and Utah
as lieutenant of the Thirteenth Infantry.
General Ka.ous was a hale and hearty
man- fend, -up to a few -weeks ago,' had
never/been ill. He was seized with grippe
and, although the malady * had not kept
him from his duties appreciably," ; he] suf
fered/ considerably. Within the. past few
days! it had been noticed that , his mind
was fellghtly affected. .. Yesterday - he was
disposed â¢ to ." be very . melancholy. Last
Sunday he manifested marked symptoms
of derangement while. In the presence of
his family. .
1 Hf 'arose at his usual time this morn-
Ingr.l about' 6:30 o'clock, . and while his
wiff 'was assisting the servant to get
his I breakfast' he went into^ the base^
memt of the â¢ house. A few minutes
ldtar, a" report of a revolver was heard.
1/lif general had shot himself through the
Heart . and death was instantaneous.
'deaf persons to hear with ease, but when
pressed by representatives of the press
for sanies and addresses he. persistently
refused to givf them and- wanted to fight.
He also: refused to make any statement
as to the ' amount and the - disposition
made of the money .'collected in his meet
ings. The Call and other papers continued
to "roast" him until his following fell off,
collections dropped to low tide and the
Impostor sought other fields for weak vic
tims.
After leaving ..Oakland he spent some
time in Salt Lake, and then proceeded to
Chicago,' where, dropping the "Eden"
scheme, he inaugurated the "Zion" move
ment; which has grown to very large pro
portions. He did not .leave California
empty-handed, however. From the sale
of photographs of himself and family, col
lections in his meetings Â«ind fees for so
called "healing," be had amassed a very
large sum of moneyâ just how much no
one on the outside knows. At any. rate,
he had the cash in hand to make large
purchases of real estate on Michigan ave
nue in Chicago and start in '.'business" on
a larger scale. The assessment rolls soon
after rated his holdings of real estate at
$251,000, which included the. Zion building
and the Zion City Bank and the land on
which they stood on Michigan avenue. He
had besides a farm in Michigan for which
he paid $10,000 cash. He had elegant car
riages and fine spans of horses with col
ored coachmen and footmen in gorgeous
livery, and altogether' made an appear
ance more In keeping with a nabob from
Special Dispatch- to The Call.
VANCOUVER; B. C. , May 21.â A ~. re
markable passion' play, ..'representing the
details of the life of Christ and his cruci
fixion, will be held . during the week of
June-3-10 at Surrey Center, in the Chilli
wack district, seventy miles in ' the in
terior from Vancouver. . The play will be
presented by 300 Indians, under the guid
ance of Father Chlrouse of the ' Catholic
church,, who will ; oversee all arrange
ments. The priest was here to-day char
tering a special train : to take the Indians
and their effects to Chilliwack-next week.
One thousand will attend from the coast
tribes between here and Alaska, and . as
many will come - from Cariboo and the
Kootenays. They . -^#1 . have (a monster
procession through ' the district, Â¦ with
chiefs in. full uniform and the' Indian
bands. from Skeena, Sechelt and other
places.; .,,-.. -â¢
Various events in the life of the' -Savior
will be illustrated in tableaux every after
noon during 'the' week and on Saturday, a
presentation of the- crucifixion will' take
place. The play will be entirely carried
out by the Indians, who for months have
been practicing for the work, and the
priest says they are now nearly perfect.
Their robes and ' the other . paraphernalia
have been purchased v by the Indians them
selves at great expense. , , V
A play somewhat similar to this was
presented by the Indians, with the help
of, white missionaries ,' several years a,go
up the- coast, but- since- then it has not
been attempted. The presentation at that
time left a lasting -impression upon the
minds of the natives, and it has been de
cided that, as a lesson full of meaning to
the heathen, it shall be repeated.

ST. LOUIS, May 21.â All records between
Euffalo and St. Louis were broken by the
Wabash special train having on board the
officers and directors of the Louisiana
Purchase Exposition, who returned to-day
from the Pan-American Exposition open
ing ceremonies. The trip from Buffalo
72C miles, was made in 14 hours 55 min
utes, counting stops and the time lost -in
ferrying across the r>etroit River. Thus
the actual running time was less than
fourteen hours, four hours shorter than
the fastest schedule between the two
cities.

COLUMBUS. Ohio. May 2Lâ The Su
preme Cami>. Woodmen of the "World, In
committee of the whole to-day considered
the report of the committee on legisla
tion. The policy of reducing, expenses is
being .adhered to and the emergency fund
Is -receiving' attention, it being decided to
day that all fees for transfers should go
Into this fund. Under the line of re
trenchment policy was the reduction of
the board of trustees from seven to five,
which -was agreed to.
This afternoon the Supreme Camp con
sidered the recommendation of the legis
lative committee to eliminate the clause
from the insurance policies which makes
ruicide annul the claim. It Is expected
that the change will be made and that
the beneficiaries of suicides may collect
the insurance.

Moffatt and Gardner Are Matched
for the Middle-Weight
Championship.
The San Francisco Athletic . Club has
matched â¢ Jack Moffatt of Chicago' and
George Gardner, the top-notch middle
weights, to fight in this city on July. 4.
According to the plans of the club, the
fight will take place during the middle of
the day at the Sixteenth and i Folsoni
street grounds, and will be for the cham
pionship of the middle-weight class.
Gardner and Moffatt have met before.
In New York several years ago. Moffatt
broke his arm on Gardner, but-the-gen
eral verdict at that time was that Moffatt
had pretty much the best of it. Gard
ner has beaten George Byers twice and has
a long record to show for his fistic career.
The San Francisco Club is â¢ also trying
to secure a second match for the. same
day. This will be a preliminary between
the winner of the Matty Matthews and
Rube Fern match and Thurston.

THE, FOURTH OF JITLY
ing of the directors of-the Chicago Board
of Trade to-day the Peavy elevator "B"
: was declared irregular pending an> inves
tigation of charges made by Slaighter.&
Co. to the effect that grain stored in the
elevator was not regular. The announce
ment caused a stir on the board, as the
action is said to be without precedent.
Following is the notice sent by Slaighter
' & Co. to Secretary Stone of the Board of
Trade:
"As the Peavy Grain Company cannot
or will not deliver No. 2 corn for which we
now hold loading orders, we hereby notify
you we will not receive as regular deliv
ery any wheat or corn in Peavy elevator
"B' on regular contracts made on the
Chicago Board of Tradei"
These charges are said to be the direct
result of the Phillips deal in May corn.
The Peavy Company, it is -said, tendered
to Phillips on delivery warehouse receipts
calling for 648,000 bushels of No. 2 corn.
Slaighter & Co. applied for this corn oh
loading orders from Phillips, but the grain
offered out of elevator "B". was refused
on the ground thai It was not up to con
tract grade. As a result of the directors'
action none of the 607,000 bushels of corn
in the elevator can be delivered and there
fora excitement was fomented in May
corn. Most of the grain in elevator *'B"
is said to have come from private Â¦ware
houses, and an investigation as to how it

JOCKEY OXAWSON IS Â¦
A VERY SICK MAN
Home . in Wisconsin " in a
Very Weak State.
PHOENIX, Ariz., May 21.â Richard
Clawson, the well-known jockey, who was
at one time in "the employ of the" W. f C.
"Whitney stables, left here last night for
Gun City, Mo., his former home, accom
panied by his wife. Clawson is a, very
sick man, his trouble being an "affecjion
of the lungs. He has been spending the
winter herein the hope of regaining his
health, and' at various . times has felt' so
much better that he thought he would
surely be all right again in the. spring.
Recently a change' for the worse took
place and he "has been losing-, strength
rapidly, and his present journey is looked
upon with anxiety. â¢ . â¢â¢ ; :'. \

DYNAMITE RATES
BANK BUILDING
Explosion Wrecks an In
stitution in State of
Massachusetts. â¢
Police Believe an Attempt
Was Made to Rob the
; :-. 'Â¦Â¦- â¢'. Vaults.
Â¦ - Â¦ . .. . .... jr
CAMBRIDGE, Mass., . May 21.â What
the police believe was an attempt to rob
the Cambridgeport National Bank of this
city, by the use of dynamite in broad day
light was revealed at 10:45 a. m. to-day by
an explosion in the bank, which blew out
the side of the building, damaging other
parts of it, arid in which Cashier William
A. Roaf sustained a wound on the head.
If the . explosion was a part of a plot
to rob the bank the plan failed, for. noth
ing of value was taken from the place.
It ie thought from the force of the con
cussion and other evidence that dynamite
was used, and that it may have been set
off by a time fuse.
The bank is in a four-story brick struc
ture in the business center of Cambridge.
The shock of- the explosion was felt for
several blocks, but no damage was done
outside of the bank building. -

HOME MISSIONS
LACK SUPPORT
General Assembly Delegates
Asked to Urge Greater
Aid.
PHILADELPHIA, May 2Lâ The com
missioners to the Presbyterian General
Assembly to-day gave vent to a sponta
neous burst of patriotic enthusiasm
which found voice in the hearty rendition
of a national anthem. The report of the
committee on home missions had bcea
read and the Rev. Charles O. Thompson,
secretary of the Home Mission Board,
arose to make the usual appeal for aid.
which developed into an oration and
aroused the assembly to the highest pitch
of enthusiasm.
A statement in , the committee's report
that over 3000 churches failed to give di
rectly to the Home Mission Board aroused
the ire of the commissioners representing
self-supported synods and presbyteries*
and after a brief but lively debate the ob
jectionable paragraph was eliminated.
The Rev. Dr. Hush K. Walker, chair
man of the committee, in discussinsr the
report declared that unless the churches
supported the home missions the foreign
missions would . fall. The report urged
Congress to act on the anti-polygamy
amendment to the constitution before thÂ©
Mormons secure the balance of political
power in any additional States. Both
morning and afternoon sessions were de
voted to routine business.
The Mohammedans want a better way
to Mecca. They desire to go there by
railway; and for that purpose have given
the Sultan $103,000 to be used In construct
ing a railroad.

WASHINGTON, May 2Lâ The Coroner's
Jury which nas been Investigating the
murder of James S. Ayres, the young
Census Office clerk, at the Hotel Ken
more last Wednesday morning, to-day
brought in the following verdict:
"James Seymour Ayres came to his
death on "Wednesday, May 15, 1901, about
the hour of 2 o'clock a. m., at the Hotel
Kenrcore, died of a pistol shot- wound of
the chest. Further, said shot was fired
during a fatal conflict between said James
Seymour Ayres and Iola Ida Henri Bo
nlne. WÂ«, the Coroner's Jury, hold Iola
Ida Henri Bonine for the Grand Jury."
Before the jury rendered its verdict a
number of witnesses were heard. An ef
fort was made to show that Miss Minas
could easily have heard voices through
the door connecting her room with that
of Ayres. and that lights could have been
seen through the- cracks. Detectives
Home and Weedon testified to this effect,
but Miss Minas declared that she saw no
lights on the night of the shooting. She
also testified ! that the cries she heard
coming from Ayres' room were made by
a. man s voice and not by a woman's. 'She
was positive that the voice was not that
of Mrs. Bonine.
This contradicts Mrs. Bonine, who in
her confession yesterday said that she
had uttered the only vocal sounds made
In the room at the time of the tragedy.
Detective Home's testimony concerning
yesterday's confession was not materially
different from the statement as then
given out District Attorney Gould said
that Mrs. Bonine had decided not to ap
pear before the Coroner's Jury.
The typewritten copy of her confession
was taken to Mrs. Bonine to-day for the
purpose of having her bwear to it, as she
was not under oath when she made the
statement yesterday. Mrs. Bonine after
making the confession had consulted a
lawyer and he advised her to avoid any
thing in the nature of assisting the prose
cution. When the confession was present
ed to her she positively refused to make
the desired affidavit.
After the Coroner's Jury had rendered
the verdict Mrs. Bonine was removed to
the City Jail.
Iola Bonine Must Answer
for James Ayres'
Death.

JURY ORDERS
THE WOMAN HELD
RYDB, Isle of Wight, May 21.â The trial
of Shamrock II and Shamrock I to-day
was in many respects the most puzzling
that has yet taken place. Instead of
throwing additional light upon the abili
ties of the challenger, to-day's race makes
it more difficult than ever to estimate her
speed.
On the stretch from Cowes to the Sll
kiker they had an interesting struggle
under full racing canvas, with the skip
pers at the helm. Officially racing has not
commenced, but the skippers were appar
ently having a trial between themselves
and snatching every advantage. The
Shamrock II was a good half-mile to the
leeward when they started, but in the five
miles between the anchorage and the Sil
klkcr she closed up the whole of this big
gap. After threatening to go through to
weather Shamrock II had to be thrown
into the wind and held all aback while
Captain Wnnge sailed . the Shamrock I
boldly across his bows on the wrong tack.
The keenness of the contest was shown
by the fact that Captain Wringe tried
this bluff, for had Captain Sycamore held
on there would probably have been a dis
astrous collision.
For the first five miles of the official
course the new yacht did equally well,
opening out on every board and then sud
denly falling off instead of gaining. The
new yacht lost on every tack. The ex
plaration.given by those on board is that
the bobstay weakened and spilled the head
sails and foresails, but it is strange if an
imperceptible difference in the trim of her
sails made so much difference in her sail*
ing.
However, as it was, the old boat turned
the weather mark nearly three minutes
ahead and went off down the wind at a
great pace. Then came another surprise,
for the challenger, which had never be
fore been able to run her, closed up all the
time, and when they finished at Nab light
ship the times were:
Shamrock I, 2 hours' 6 minutes and 12
seconds. . â¢. . : - â¢
Shamrock II, 2 hours 11 minutes and 19
seconds. *.
The opinion of most of the experts who
witnessed the' race was that had it been
desired that the ' Shamrock II should win
she could easily have done so.
RYDE, May 21.â King Edward will 7 wit
ness to-morrow's race between t. the two
Shamrocks.. - .' t . â¢ .. -'
SHAMROCK II A PUZZLE.
Race With Shamrock I Gives No
light as to Her Ability.
BRISTOL, R. I.. May 21.â Up and down
Narragansett Bay to-day the cup defender
CCnstitutlon was put through her first
paces and acquitted herself, as ilr. Dun
can.' her manager, said, most satisfac
torily.
Starting out this forenoon In a dying out
westerly breeze, she showed herself an ex
cellent drifter, while in- the afternoon a
fine southwester sent her scudding about
the bay in a lively fashion, with little fuss
and scarcely any feathery foam under her
smooth, bow or in her clean wake. Every
point of sailing was tried. She was hauled
flat on the wind, thrown about half a
dozen times, given a grood broad reach,
and finally sent home before the wind
with her boom off to port, and at no time
did she act listlessly or show any lack of
It was a builders' trial and confined to
six or seven miles of deep water below
Bristol harbor, no attempt being made to
show her off to the Newport colony, but
merely to give her designer a chance to
see that she was all he meant her to be,
and in every respect she equaled his ex
pectations.

Dr. Bunker's Examination.
The preliminary examination of Dr.
Robert E. Bunker on the charge of mur
der in connection with the death of Jane
Bouillaguet. a governess, was continued
befcre Judge Cabanlss yesterday after
noon. The prosecution closed with the
evidence of Dr. .Bacigalupi, autopsy phy
sician. Attorney Hosmer then made a
motion to dismiss. He argued that Miss
A. Cerles, the principal witness for the
prosecution, was. If anything, an accom
plice If a crime had been committed and
ihere was nothing to corroborate her tes
timony. The Judge denied the motion and
the defendant testified in his own behalf.
Dr. W. R. Cluness also testified for the
defense, and the case was continued Ull
this morning.
While every one Is preparing to decorate
the graves of the soldiers who served the
country so well and faithfully there is a
deep-founded desire to pay some special
tribute to the men who fought on the sea
and who lie sleeping beneath its restless
waves. For years it has been the custom
to decorate the mounds of earth that
mark the last resting place of the soldier
and to include in the decoration a thought
to the memory of the sailor, but this year
and hereafter it is proposed to strew flow
ers on the breast of old ocean In special
honor and memory of the dead he has
clasped in his great white arms.
The suggestion cprnes from Mrs. Arml
tage S. C. Forbes. It has received the In
dorsement of every one who has heard of
It, and particularly of men of the navy.
A year ago such a demonstration was
made, but It was quietly done and only
by those who had good cause to show
some special care for such a ceremony.
This year, however, the idea has claimed
attention from many sources and the out
going tide will bear with It garlands and
wieaths and big bunches of blossoms
dropped on the surface of tho sea In
tribute to the memory of those who were
its sons and those it has claimed as its
own.
In years gone by the sailor dead have
not been neglected. The mournful mounds
that tell of their last berth ashore have
briEhtened at the close of every May
with the flowers loving hands have show
ered over them, but a peaceful rest under
the daisies is not the only fate of those
who brave the storm and carry the flag
through wind and weather. â¢
The history of the country is brightened

General Lacayo Pays a Fine.
General Carlos Lacayo, ex-Consul Gen
eral of Nicaragua, was convicted In Judge
Fritz's court yesterday on the charge of
disturbing the peace and was fined $50.
Early last Thursday morning he assaulted
Oeorpe Brown, a student from Chile, at
Powell and O'Farrell streets because
Brown asked him for money loaned. The
general claimed that Brown made dispar
aging remarks about one of his lady
friends.

while the living cleared,, for action once
more. It Is one thing to fight with the
solid old mother earth beneath the feet
it is. another to fight with a thousand
fathoms of clutching, clawing sea reach
ing for ship and man, where defeat means
to sink with the vessel and where even
victory has often meant the same. For
these men there has been no special trib
ute on Memorial day. but it is the inten
tion that they shall have one.
And the men who stand to the guns are
not the only toilers for the flag at sea.
There are thousands more who carry It
to the farthest confines of the world
bringing thte message It bears and the
treasures from the land it represents.
Memorial day has grown to be more than
a time of memory for the man who bore
arms, and next to the man from the war
ship s deck la the merchant man, who
creates in peace what the bluejacket pro
tects in war. The annals of the merchant
marine are full of records of "lost In a
storm. Scarcely a week passes without
some vessel coming In through the Golden
Gate with her flae at half-mast and one
man missing from her me^; and this Is
h U Li?J?J Â°Â£ V 16 , c ? un try's seaports. The
howling wind j picks as many men from
tho royal yard as the bullet takes from
the firing line, and the sea gets them. all.
A man may drop In his tracks with a bul
let through his breast and the surgeon
gets him, but a man from the yanfarm
plunging headlong Into the smother of
foam and spray is gone forever. He too
should have at. least one blossom on the
hC A av i n fu breast of tne sea. that keeps him

Lacy flrst met Mr. Burleigh, and subse
quently Introduced on this coast the drill
which bears the name of the inventor.
Mr. Lacy began his business career In San
Francisco in 1871. He was born at Wex
ford, Ireland, fifty-five years ago.
The deceased leaves a wife and two sons
and daughters to mourn him. - , .Â¦ .
ceased had suffered but a short time.
Benjamin T. Lacy was a man of pro
gressive energy. He was connected with
the work of drilling the Mount Cenis tun
nel through the Alps and of boring the
Hoosac tunnel In New England before he
came to California. He also performed
important work under General Newton in
Congressman Loud also deems the com
ing visit of the committee to be of much
public Interest In California. The commit
tee, he eays, will visit the Atlantic and
Gulf ports and river systems as well as
the waterways of the Pacific slope, Its
Idea being to get a complete knowledge of
the entire subject as it concerns the
country at large. Very likely It will visit
the great lakes, if the members are not
thoroughly acquainted with them already.
He understands the committee will travel
at its own expense.
Congressman Julius Kahn has received
advises from Washington that lead him
to think that nearly all the members of
the Congressional Committee on Rivers
and Harbors will come to California next
morlh. He 6ald yesterday that before he
left the East for home he had a talk with
Congressman Burton, who is the chair
man of the House committee, concerning
the journey of the committee members,
and he thinks it probable that their visit
to California may have a very beneficial
effect. This State had large interests
reptesented in the provisions of the river
and harbor bill as it was reported from
the committee at the last session. Among
the items was one of $612,000 for the im
provement of Oakland harbor. The San
Joaquin and Sacramento rivers were to
be benefited by the expenditure of sums
aggregating $250,000. There is also a rec
ommendation for the disbursement of
S216.000 unexpended money for the work
on f.hag rock and Arch rock to secure a
depth of thirty-four feet of water over
them- Redwood and Petaluma creeks
also are in the line of improvement. Alto
gether the amount that may be expended
for the betterment of Callfgornla rivers
and harbors is about $1,600,000.
"The purpose of the coming of the Riv
ers and Harbors Committee," said Con
gressman Kahn, "is to gain personal
knowledge of the waterways and to find
out what Is needed, that means may be
provided to give what relief can be af
forded. I assume that a majority of the
committee will come to the Coast. From
Congressman Burton I have learned that
he has tried to enlist the Interest of all
the members of the committee. The mat
ter is of importance. I will accompany
the committee, if it desires, when it ar
rives, and help it to get all the knowledge
that It needs of the various localities
where improvements may be made, and
also of waterways generally."
Benjamin T. Lacy, Pioneer Mining Implement
Dealer, Dies at His Home.

It Is Impossible for this court to say that the
showing of such a violation of a city ordi
nance did not prejudice the Jury before whom
ho waÂ» being- tried. â¢ â¢ â¢ In view of these
facts the court reverses the Judgment and order
of the lower court and remands the case for a
new trial.
The Supreme Court handed down a de
cision yesterday reversing the judgment
and order of the Superior Court. -; which
convicted Thomas A. Kelly a year and a
half ago of the charge â¢ of conspiring to
defraud a public " office. . Â¦Â¦ '",
Kelly was accused of accepting "straw"
bonds as ball â¢ for the recognizance of a
negro sneak thief named "Wilson, who fled
froih the city as soon as he was freed. : It
was alleged that Kelly accepted the fraud
ulent bonds knowingly and had. made an
agreement to do so with Wilson's attor
ney, J. N. E. Wilson. .
In rendering the decision ;the, Supreme
Court considered but two points In the
appeal; first, that there was a large
amount of extraneous evidence admitted
at the trial, and second, that Judge Law
lor, before whom the case was tried, had
wrongfully charged the jury. v. .
It was admitted that evidence In regard
to conversations between Wilson, the ac
cused, and his attorney, at which Kelly
was not proven to have been present, had
been allowed to be presented, by the
Judge, although it Incriminated the pris
oner.- The Supreme Court therefore -held
that a conviction under, such circum
stances was illegal. In charging the jury
the former court had read an ordinance
wherein it is prohibited to a police court
clerk to accept ball bonds outside the
limits of the City Hall. The trial court
then admitted its error, but said there was
evidence enough aside from the ordinance
to convict.
In reference to this point the Supreme
Court says: ;
a Jury.

The club is also planning to have a club
house as soon as feasible and unless
articles of Incorporation are filed Its of
ficials cannot transact business in a
proper manner. Mrs. White also states
that the club has some money and it Is
desired to invest this in such a manner as
to draw more Interest than It does at pres
ent. It is thought that as a business con
cern the club can inspire more respect
among business men than If it remains an
irresponsible organization. Every one is
strongly In favor of the plan, and Mrs.
"White states that there Is : no doubt /but
what it will be adopted at the regular
business meeting.
After the meeting the members were ad
dressed by Mrs. Mary Urquhart Lee,- the
distinguished parliamentarian, from the
East, on the economic and social condi
tions of the Mormon women. L. M. King
spoka on the subject. of street cleaning.
The clubrooms were taxed to their full
capacity and the greatest Interest was
shown by the members in the topics un
der discussion. At the conclusion of the
addresses numerous questions were asked
the speakers. .
" Mrs. Lee gave an Interesting sketch of
a number of the most prominent of tlie
Mormon women and attributed to them a
high degree of Independence and self-re
liance. She asserted . that their &ssoci>
tion with the men In political life had
greatly developed them and that as a rule
they had become more determined and
more willing to face the drudgery of llfÂ«
than the ordinary woman. She spoke of
the remarkable skill they have attained
as Â¦weavers of silk and gave a very en
tertaining description of silk culture in
general. â¢ '
By unanimous -vote Mrs. J. J; Scovllle,
Mrs. Aaron Schloss. Mrs. J. S. Spear, Mrs.
Dorothy Harnden and Mrs. Charles. A.
"Warren were appointed a committee to
learn how the action might best be taken
and to report thereon at the regular an
nual business. meeting of the club, which
Is to be held on next Tuesday.
Mrs. Lovell "White originated "the plan
more than a year ago and the matter has
been discussed frequently since that time,
but no public attention was given to it till
yesterday. The incentive to Incorporate,
Mrs. White says, arises from a general
desire to increase the scope and efficacy of
the work of the club. ;Â¦Â»
Mrs. Lovell White, president of the
club, addressed the ladies present on the
subject of incorporating the organization.
The project met' with favor. Suggestions
were made" 1 by some Denver ladies who
are visiting 1 In San Francisco and who are
members of an incorporated club in their
home city. Â¦.-They- stated' that with them
the experiment had proved an 'unques
tioned success and placed their club on. a
much higher basis.

Mrs. Lindsay, Johnson's Efforts.
It is a far cry from William Morris and
the Kelmscott shop to the Georgia moun
taineer at Fouches Gap, and yet an arts
and crafts movement has been initiated

of years. Their Ignorance explains their

occasional fruitless industry, speak of
greater capabilities within them. â¢ 'Â¦

" Fostering Hand Work In America. Â¦
True it is that the Occidental nations
have ever lacked that subtle necromancy
of touch which makes of the products of
the Orient wonders of the world. It Is
equally true that our own new world has
found neither place nor time for the beau
tics of European hand work, but for this
latter deficiency there have been reasons
which do not, perhaps, argue a lack of
artiwtlc possibilities. Though the. hand
products of tha western world may never
equal those of tha Orient they may cer
tainly attain an admirable degree of
ixauty and-excellonce and be. found ox*
prooslve of our life and civilization.
The General Federation of Women's
Clubs has a strong central committee,
which has done much to stimulate inter
est in and desire for hand products. Its
chairman, Mrs. Herman J. Hall of Chi
cago, with an able corps of assistants, ar
ranged at the last biennial convention of
women's clubs in Milwaukee an exhibit
ol arts and crafts which was of unex
celled value and significance of interest.
The handwork of ail nations appeared tn
wonclerf ul â¢ and dazzling array, Â¦ metal,
Jewels, -wood, lace, leather and all the
wonderful weaves of the Orient testifying
to the art of the old world' craftsman.
Few and pale in comparison seemed the
rugs and pots, the draperies and the carv
ings which stood for "home products,"
but of unspeakable Interest to the loyal
American, who saw in them the promise
of unfolding beauty and development both
of the art and the artist.
Work by the General Federation.
'.- Social telesis, as a great social philos
opher has aptly named this effort of.so
clety.faa a whole,' to assist socialevolu
tion, is manifesting itself more andr^e
strongly in afl the movements of the <*W,
and such a group as is constituted by the
club women of the United States should be
a potent factor in observing and fostering
social tendencies, v
A notable instance in this direction is to
be found in the great and growing inter
est 'of American club women in tne new
movement toward democratic art and its
expression, which is manifesting itself so
strongly in the United States to-day.
Never before has there been such a ten
dency to make art a part of the dally life
of all the people and to draw from the
simplest individuals some product of the
hand inspired by the individual mind.
The club women of America have done
and are doing much to foster this ten
dency for individual hand production,
which is raising- its head feebly enough
amid the endless profusion of machine
made products. ' : ':Â¦Â¦â¢
The work of women's clubs falls under
two headsâ what they do and what they
suggest. Â¦
Every discussion must take into consid
eration these two distinct lines of influ
ence and there can be little question that
the abstract field of suggestion is quite as
important as the concrete one of actual
endeavor. ;
.Society Is only Just reaching that stage
in evolution when it perceives Its "ten
dencies" and consciously strives to foster
them.

Linens and Silk Scrap Draperies.
Some beautiful hand-woven linens have
been produced for sheets and towels, but
the cost of the flax necessitates so consid
erable an Investment that this feature of
the industry must needs wait on orders or
further capital.
Good success has attended ' the weaving
of the silk scrap draperies which axe so
effective for portieres, dlyan covers and
similar uses. The same 'product which
can be woven in New York for $1 a yard
can be woven in the mountains of North
Georgia for about 10 cents a vard. In
each case the silk scraps are, of course,
furnished by the customer.
Mrs. Nellie Peters Black of Atlanta,
whose farming interests also border on
the mountain district, has found similar
interests to foster. Â¦ Â¦ -
From Pine Log come, besides the weav
ing, old-fashioned silk quilts, hand-made
hats, and some interesting specimens of
basketry in which cornstalks, pine needles
and broomstraw are used with really ex
cellent effect. . .
Exhibits of all these products are made
not only at the State fairs, which are the
best markets, but at the various women's
clubs of the State, and thus a steady and
increasing demand for the hand wares
has been produced, which has- proved
mutually advantageous. to producer and
consumer. One must needs know the
homes and - the home life of the women
who do the handweaving to realize what
the coming of ready cash must mean to
them.

gtrls 70 years young."
Although the mountain women are
those for whom â¢ southern club women

mena of burnt wood, carved and burnt

Work of Negro Craftsmen.
The work of some negro craftsmen has
been encouraged. ; They have done par
ticularly well in basketry, mainly in large
pieces, such as clothes hampers and waste
baskets made of willow withes. These are
pretty In white and when stained with
analine dyes, in bright reds and greens,
they give a picturesque Indian effect.
That so little of that effect appears In our,
art products to-day marks the crowning
loss Of American crafts.
That the wonders of -Indian pottery and
basketry, at once so unique and so char
acteristic, should have been permitted to
lapse and disappear from the art of our
nation was an error as inexplicable as it
waa irreparable. The remains of.,thls
aboriginal art may be stimulated some
what in. the "West and Northwest, but in
the South, where "the red man -in some
what isolated Instances appeared within
the writer's recollection,- no trace is left
of his crafts, .the secrets of which should
In an> important respect the Nantucket lightship
service differs from what has been done hitherto. The
former enterprises made use of wireless telegraphy for
temporary purposes; it was applied only for special
occasions. The service now established by the Herald
is to be permanent. It is to continue until science and
invention have devised better methods. At present it
represents the best in*the world and will be of vast
benefit to the public. A saving of fifteen hours in
getting news from trans-Atlantic steamers is an event
of high importance, and the whole commercial world
may be congratulated on the enterprise of our New
York contemporary.
By reason of its relations with the Herald % The Call
will have the benefit of the improved service, and thus
will again be a sharer in the advance made in tele
graphing without wires. It will be remembered that
The Call was the first paper to apply wireless teleg
raphy to the service of newsgatheringwhen it obtained
from a vessel off the Golden Gate the announcement
of the arrival of the transport bringing home the Cal
ifornia volunteers from the Philippines. The next
notable work with wireless telegraphy was done when
the Herald and The Call reported the international
yacht races off Sandy Hook from a steamer that fol
lowed the yachts and dispatched reports of every fea
ture of the race as it occurred, thus getting the news
far ahead of papers that relied upon the old methods.
BY the New York Herald there has been estab
lished on Nantucket lightship a system of wire
less telegraphy, by which news of incoming
steamers from Europe will be received fifteen hours
earlier than at present. A gain of that much in time
is an important factor to all interests that are affected
by steamer news, and consequently the establishment
of the improved service is one of the most notable
things that have been accomplished thus far by the
wirfcless method of telegraphing.

How an Enthusiast Was Disappointed
I remember one rather amusing Incident
of dashed expectancy in the case of a club
Â¦woman : who was an 'ardent proraulga tor
of the arts and crafts. movement and was
constantly on the watch for hopeful ma
terial from which to develop a craftsman.
One day a servant brought to her bed
room a tabouret mado of bamboo and
matting, but executed by the hand of a
skilled workman. He reported that a man
at the door wished to sell It. The club
woman was all interest at once. A Geor
gia countryman who could make a tabou
ret like that was a treasure. Buying the
llttlo specimen, she cent word to tho
maker to como back with other work
when she could see him. The wife of the
American Minister to Japan was present
and Paid mildly that it looked very like
Japanese workmanship. The club woman,
however, protested that there was no rea
pon why every good pleoe of Joiner's worlt
done in bamboo should be necessarily
Japanese*'' Â¦ â¢
It was a blow when the craftsman ap
peared a few days later and proved to bo
a full-blooded Japanese to whom English
was still an unknown quantity.
The club woman took her disappoint
ment generously, ordering fascinating 1 tea
table* and porch chairs for a summer
cottage. Sundry younjr American crafts
men, seeing the work, were Induced to
emulate It.
Growing Demand for Sand Work.
There can be little doubt that this move
ment for hand work, with Its excellenco
of execution and Its Individual expression.
Is to be more than a passing fad in Amer
ica, That a market is to be afforded
among us Is evident from such facts aÂ»
the well known one that the ateliers of
numerous artists of to-day have been
turned Into workshops, -with orders for
hand-made furniture pressing for execu
tlcn.
The same demand seems easy to creats
In every department of the hand crafts,
and In creating this demand and stimu
lating the production which shall, with
increasing excellence, fill it, lies a great
field for Interesting 1 /and valuable effort
on the part of the American club woman.

During a recent epidemic of diphtheria
in a town on the Hudson. 205 cases were
treated with serum, and among these
there were only two deaths. ,
The Democratic Review for November, 1839, re
commended that canal treaties be made with the
Central American governments and England, France,
Holland and Russia. Clayton made the first of these,
with England, now known as the Clayton-Bulwcr
treaty, and yet the project is but little farther advanced
than when the monk united the oceans in the ravine
of Raspadura, one hundred and fourteen years ago.
In 1835 John M. Clayton promoted an inquiry
in Congress, and the result was an exhaustive report
with appendix containing the opinion of Mr. Radcliff,
to the effect that what is now known as the Panama
route was the preferable route. /* :
' The next effort was a grant to Baron de Thierry to
build a canal by aid of the rivers Chagres and Grande
and the Bay of Limon. Santander/ President of New
Granada, made this contract. De Thierry was an usher
in Cambridge University v and in that capacity met
some chiefs from New Zealand, who, in a freak, con
ferred upon him letters patent as "Sovereign Chief of
New Zealand." When he solicited the canal grant from
Santander he insisted upon the use of that title, and
when it was not employed his zeal for the canal abated.
He was a mere adventurer. This brings the history of
the canal up to 1835 and the organization of Biddle's
company and its failure.
Simon Bolivar, the Liberator, while ruler of Co
lombia, gave much attention to a canal. He employed
Lloyd, ah Englishman, and Falmarck, a Swede, both
engineers, to investigate the subject, and they reported
in England to the Royal Society and the Royal Geo
graphical Society. Bolivar, at Guayaquil, in 1829, de
clared to an American gentleman that, having brought
the war between Colombia and Peru to a victorious
conclusion, he intended, on his return to Bogota, to
employ his army in building the canal. But Bolivar
was compelled by failing health to resign and soon
after died. It is believed that, had he lived and con
tinued in power, the employment of his tremendous
energy and talents would have assured the completion
of the work.
After this failure President Jackson sent Mrv Bid
die to Central America to negotiate for a canal. But
Biddle was unequal to the task arid his mission was a
mortifying failure.
Next a Dutch company ,wns formed under the
patronage' of the King of Holland. General Vcrvecr
was sent by the King to Central America. This project
was defeated by the Belgian revolution.
After their separation from Spain the Central
American governments began immediately to con
sider an isthmian canal. September 18, 1824, the En
glish house of Barclay & Co., representing a company
composed of American merchants, made proposals to
Central America for the construction of a canal by the
San Juan and Lake Nicaragua. This proposition seems
to have aborted, but in June, 1826, Charles de
Beneski, representing American capitalists, made a
contract for a canal by that route. The contract set
forth that the canal should admit merchant ships of
the largest class and be open to all nations on equal
terms. Possessed of the contract, Mr. Palmer exe
cuted a deed of trust to DffWitt Clinton, Stephen Van
Rensselaer, C. D. Colden, Philip Hone and Lynde
Catlin, by which they were made commissioners to
superintend the organization of the "Central, American
and United States Atlantic and 'Pacific Canal Com
pany." A financial crisis, and political disturbances on
the isthmus, killed that project.
The fifth was by the same river Atrato and its trib
utary, the Quito. The waters of the latter approach
near to a stream called the San Juan; emptying into
the Pacific. Between the two is a small ravine called
De la Raspadura... Humboldt says that a monk of
great enterprise, cure of the village of Novita, em
ployed his parishioners to dig a small canal in that
ravine, through which, and by the rivers, canoes
loaded with cacao pass from sea to sea, and that this
communication between the oceans was in operation
in 1788, and was unknown in Europe!
The fourth was by the river Atrato, which, witl\ its
tributary, the Napipi, is navigable to within eighteen
miles of the Bay of Cupica on the Pacific.
Humboldt, in his "Political Essay on the Kingdom
of New Spain," enumerated five different routes for
the canal. The first is within the limits of Mexico,
from the Bay of Tehuantepec ' on the Pacific to the
Bay of Campeachy on the Gulf, following the rivers
Chimalapa and Huasacualco, whose navigable waters
approach to within twenty miles of each other. The
second was the Nicaragua route by the San Juan River
and Lake Nicaragua. The third was by the. Isthmus
of Panama, requiring a canal only thirty miles long,
and was most favored by Humboldt.
â¢ Again, in 1788, Jefferson wrote: "With respect to
the Isthmus of Panama, I am assured by Burgoin that
a survey was made; that a canal "was practicable, and
that the idea was suppressed for political reasons alto
gether. He has seen and minutely examined the re
port. This report seems to me a vast desideratum for
reasons practical and philosophical."
THE proposition for an isthmian canal, to connect
the Atlantic and Pacific oceans, is by no means
new. It did not even originate in the nineteenth
century. It had birth long before the age of railroads,
and preceded the adoption of steam power on the water.
The Edinburgh Review of January, 1809, spoke of it as
"the mightiest event in favor of the peaceful inter
course^ nations which the physical circumstances of
the globe presents to the enterprise of man." But at
that date the discussion of the canal was far advarfecd.
In 1787 Jefferson, 1 then our Minister to France, in a
letter to Mr. William Carmichael at Madrid said: "I
have been told that cutting through the Isthmus of
Panama, which the world has so often wished and
supposed practicable, has at times been thought of by
the Government of Spain, and" that they proceeded
once so far as to have a survey and examination made
of the ground; but that the result was either imprac
ticability or too great difficulty."

THE SAN FEA^CISCp CALL, WEDNESDAY, ;MAZ; 22, 19( >1.
Whatever view be taken of the responsibility of par
ticular individuals, it is certainly an evil that a few men
are able artificially in a time of prosperity to bring
about a panic of such large proportions. There should
be some way to fix the responsibility definitely and
then provide means'of restraining such an exercise of
power hereafter. In this case the injury was confined
to speculators, but in a time of industrial depression or
financial weakness a similar panic might affect the
whole country.
The Boston Herald attributes the crash to a set of
men who it says are simply stock gamblers. They have
no desire to control a railroad and when they purchase.
stock they do so solely for speculative purposes.
"These men," it says, "came to the conclusion that
there was a fight for control on foot between tre
mendous financial interests, and that the opportunity,
under such circumstances, for a 'corner' in the stock
was uncommonly good. The speculators who then
entered into the market as purchasers were unques
tionably the true cornerers, and they had not the least
compunction in exacting from the unfortunate seller
every ounce of flesh and blood that the terms of the
contract gave them the right to demand. It was this
outside participancy in the movement, that had not
been entirely foreseen by the two rival financial in
terests, which led to the seriousness of last week's
crisis.
There is an evident unwillingness on the part of
most of the critics of the event to name the "eminent
financiers" who are supposed to have been responsible
for it all. The men who were struggling to get posses
sion of Northern Pacific stock so as to control the
road have one and all denied having any complicity in
the corner. It is stated that two banking houses, those
of J. P. Morgan & Co. and of'Kuhn, Loeb & Co., held
between them, either in actual possession or in prom
ises to deliver, a larger number of shares than was
represented by the entire capital stock of the Northern
Pacific road, and consequently they could have formed
a corner and cinched the street had they desired. Still
there seems no reason to believe they did so, for, in
stead of taking advantage of the situation, they are
reported to have been prompt in trying to arrange a
means of relieving those who were caught.
THE CORNER AND THE CLIQUE.
HAVING studied out the recent corner in North
ern Pacific and reached the conclusion that the
whole flurry, fiom first to last, was absolutely
artificial, the people of the East arc now trying to dis
cover who arc the men that brought it about. It ap
pcart to be generally conceded that a comparatively
small number of persons organized the scheme,
worked it up, excited the speculators of the street and
then, at their own time and in their own way, precipi
tated the slump. They made some millions of money
for themselves, but they ruined an untold number of
persons who were credulous enough to risk their small
earnings in the gamble.
In commenting upon the subject the New York
Sun says: "The sudden tumble in prices on Thursday
was catised by efforts specially made by a few men to
enrich themselves through sacrificing public interests
by whipping up a storm for which nothing in the
actual conditions afforded justification. It was a panic
of purely artificial creation, of preconcerted and knav
ish manipulation, an example of the havoc which
treachery can work in the sensitive world of finance."
The United States Investor says: "The responsibility
for the appalling shake-up of the last few days can be
placed at the doors of a set of eminent financiers." ' It
adds that their immense private and corporate re
sources and their control of the banking facilities of
the country have enabled them "to embark upon the
most audacious schemes of self-aggrandizement, in the
prosecution of which they have forced upon 80,000,000
people the most terrible financial collapse on record."

A CHANGED NAVAL SERVICE.
VAST as is the contrast between the magnificent
ship that bears the name Ohio in our new fleets
and the Ohio of the old days, it by no means
represents ail the changes that the passage of time and
the improvements of life have brought about in our 5
navy/ There has been in the serviceMtself a better and
a mightier change than that in. the structure of the
ships. The new Ohio is to uphold a higher American
ism among her officers and crew. than was known to
those who sailed the older one.
. Of course, data obtained in the Adirondacks affords
no. basis for estimating the productiveness of forests
in California, where the climate,the soil and the trees
are different, but.it may be safely said that if scientific
silviculture can be made profitable in New York, equal
care and skill will make it even more profitable : here.
The 'New York experiment, therefore, can be made
of .value to , us. It shows what can be done in^that
way arid should arouse in California a resolve to emu
late the example. -; ! .-
Further interesting information concerning the rate
of growth of trees in the Adirondacks is given in the
following statement: "Of the sixty maples on which
rings were counted. a diameter of twelve inches on the
stump wa3 reached b'y seven within the limits of age
from 116 to 183 years. Ten trees reached a diameter
of sixteen inches in from 172 to 240 years, and seven-,
teen trees reached a diameter of twenty- inches in 175
to 233 years, while the oldest and stoutest reached
twenty-eight inches in 280 years. Thi3 was a domi
nant tree, and probably always had favorable position,
making one inch in the average^ every ten years for
a long time, and probably representing the best that
could be expected.. The few beech that were counted
all indicate a rate similar to the maple, the best mak
ing one inch in ten to eleven years, but they are not
growingjpersistently, the largest one reaching, a dia
meter 'of , twenty-four inches. It should be stated,
however, that this particular plot was rather poor in
beech. Birch, on the other hand, showed a better
rate in all the trees counted, namely, mostly under
ten years for one inch. The oldest and stoutest, 300
years old,~measured thirty-seven inches on the stump
with a' height of about" eighty-five feet to the. tip, fur
nishing, however, only two sound logs of twenty-eight
and twenty-nine. inches diameter with nearly 800 -feet
B: M. birch of thirty-six inches diameter
and'ninety feet height showed 275 rings. '

The New York college has contracted its wood sup
ply fo a cooperage company,, which is erecting near
one of the lakes in the Adirondacks a stave and land
ing factory to utilize the logs and a wood alcohol plant
to use. the cordwood. . The trees are felled and the
logs' cut and skidded by the employes of the college,
as it is feared the contractors would not do that work
with sufficient care for the preservation of young
trees. So careful is the economy practiced in the
work that the managers expect to be able to find, a
market for every product of the woods down to brush
one inch in diameter.

jl ship Company's Coptic sailed for
|1 the Orient yesterday. There was
H a big crowd of Chinese down to

Yung Lun was, up to Saturday night
last, a female impersonator in the Chinese
theater on Jackson street. He was paid
a high salary, but fan tan and lottery
kept him always in debt. Lun finally
came to the conclusion that he would
never be able lo pay what he owned, so
he quietly packed his trunk and pur
chased a ticket for China on the Coptic.
Vhcn he attempted to board the eteam
eh'p yesterday his creditors grabbed him
and a free fight followed, in which Yung
Lun lost part of his queue. The customs
officers interfered, and as there wag no
warrant for the arrest of the debtor they
Insisted on his beingr allowed to board the
ship. It was then Yung Lun showed his
meanness, as he 6ecured a prominent po
sition aft and laughed and Jeered at his
angry creditors as the Coptic moved away
from the wharf.

Colonel A. E. Buck is United States
Minister to Japan. He came home on a
visit a few months ago, but was attacked
by the grip and has not yet recovered
from the disease. He expects that the
pea voyage and the 'change of climate
will completely restore him to health,
however. V.
Has Troubles of His Own.
They are not a happy family in the
cabin of the scnocner Robert Searles. now
on her way from Honolulu for Puget
Sound. The vessel left here last year,
and Captain Plltz took his wife along with
him. The lady's brother wanted to go to
gea, so the captain shipped him as cabin
boy. All went well for a month or so,
and then the lad decided that his brother
in-law should treat him as one of the
family and not as one of the crew. Mrs.
Plltz sided with her brother, but the cap
tain could not see It in that light. In con
sequence, the gallant skipper had to take
many a curtain lecture. Things reached
a climax at Honolulu. There Captain
Plltz discharg-ed his brother-in-law and
shipped another cabin boy. On May 10
he sailed for Port Townsend, but hove
to off Diamond Head. The America llaru
did not bring the end of the story, but
the supposition is that Mrs. Piltz won the
day and that the captain sent ashore for
his brother-in-law and Is bringing him
home as a cassenccr.
Coloma Home Again.
The old bark Coloma arrived from Sali
nas Cruz in ballast yesterday after a
tmart round trip. She left Eureka on Feb
ruary 4 with a load of lumber and made
the run down the coast in twenty-eight
days. It took forty days to get rid of her
carg-o and make the vessel ready for sea,
and It took her thirty-eight days to -come
home. The Coloma is now thirty-two
years old, and the greater portion of her
life has been on the Pacific Coast. She is
6till sound and has a long lease of life
ahead of her.

NEW. YORK STOCK LIST. â¢
Shares . . , â¢ Â¦ Â¦ Closing
Sold. Stockâ : Bid.
The nature of the business is Indicated by
the example of several stocks to-day. Union
Pacific was sold yesterday on reports of an
additional issue of convertible "bonds to finance
the Northern Pacific stock purchase. To-day
the fact was confirmed that the company had
applied to the stock exchange to list the ad
ditional bonds, yet Union Pacific rose at one
time 4% over lart night. -Missouri Pacific' was
weak yesterday on the statement that th^ ex
pected dividend - would . not be declared at
once: to-day the directors met without acting
on the dividend, yet the stock rose four points
over the early low point. In other words, {he
bears of yesterday were covering their con
tracts to-day. The buying by railroads and
capitalists to effect changes in control of va
rious railroad systems which was supposed to
make up the bulk of the buying in the boom
market, when dealings ran between 2,000,000
and 3,000,000 shares a day, is also inconspicu
ous, although rumors of such dealings con
tinue to play some part, especially in Union
Pacific, which :s credited by . the newest ru
mors with Securing an interest in St. Paul.
The sttength of the Toledo, St. Louis and
Western stocks is accompanied by intimations
about tbe exchange that the buying u for
Vanderbilt account with a view to elimina
tion of competition. The other story of a
consolidation of Southwestern roads wns re
vived to-day in connection with the rally,* In
MlsEouri Pccific. The continuation of the In
junction agsinst Amalgamated Copper's mer.
ger projects accounted for the early weakness
of that stock. American Linseed and National
Lead were , strong, rather strangely, since the
absorption of thÂ« Linseed company by the
Union Lead and Oil Company is supposed to
indicate Increased competition for the National
Lead Company. The greater . ease of money
to-day had some Influence In the recovery In
prices. . Banks which were below their legal
reserve by Saturday's statement apparently
completed their â¢ recuperation yesterday. The
payments of an installment of subscriptions on
the Union Pacific bond issue and on the *pur
chase of the Rio Grande Western by the t>en
ver and Rio Grande which were made yester
dav resulted in some Influence on the mar
ket . to-day. No gold was engaged to-day, but
pome gold is expected to go out before the end
of the week. The â¢ stiffness of the London
money rate lifted the sterling rate at Paris
a fraction, but any relaxation In the London
money rate finds Immediate response In a de
cline in the Paris sterling rate, which means
shipments of gold from New York to Paris for
London account. That Is to say, Paris money
lent in London is called home to meet the
exigency of the Russian loan unless the Lon
don rate is kept high enough to make a loan
there rr.ore attractive.
The bond market was dull and inclined to
weakness .early, but showed some recoveries
later in sympathy with stocks. Total Bales,
par \*alue, $2,790,(00. Â¦ . .
United States new 4s declined % per cent
on ' the last call.
Â¦ The dealings were almost purely professional
and even that clars of operators showed less
interest than yesterday. There - is not much
difficulty experienced by them in moving
prices, declines being easily effected on light
offerings and advances are as easily achieved
by a smart bidding up process. The light
chare of the public in the market, however,
offers ro medium to take profits and the pro
fessionals find more and more that in cover
ing short sales or In selling to realize on pur
chases prices are brought back to about
where they started from.

ward movement, and lifted prices to a much
higher plane, except for a stock here and
thero, which did not move hi the late trading.

"Evaporated apples are firmer, 6e being

York indicate a light crop this year."
"Teaches are firm, with a continued Inquiry
for low-grade fruit at 4%c. Apricots are firm
and selling fairly well. There is a steady
Mnall' demand for 40-50s, SO- 60s, 60-7Os and 90
100s prunes.
"A little more interest is noted In Calttornia

particularly bare of 4-crown. Some inquiry
la noted for 2-crown California layers.
Mail advices from New York say: "Currants
are very firm, but the demand from the
country is only moderate, sales being chiefly
in 10-barrel' lots, with an occasional order for
a lartrer quantity. Stocks in jobbers' hands
generally are not large, but the trade is not
inclined to pay the advance, particularly at
this season of the year. Stocks in this market
will be increased by about 2500 barrels about
the middle cf June by the arrival of the Aisa
tia, which is reported . to have sailed- from
Patras on Tuesday.

COAST AND BAY SECTIONSâ The weather
durins the week has been favorable for the

SACRAMENTO VALLEYâ Cool weather dur
ing the week has been favorable for all crops.
Light showers have fallen in portions of the
valley, but the rain has not interfered with
hay-n-.aiing. which iÂ« row progressing rapidly.
<ira:n has made a remarkable improvement
since the recent rains and in some places the
yield will be nearly double the amount antici

Nearly normal temperatures have prevailed
throughout the State and the weather has been
irenerally cloudy or foggy in . must . . sections.

fornia and In portions of the Sacramento Val
ley. Rain is falling to-da>* in the coast and

Northern California. Conditions during the
week have been very favorable for all crops,
though slightly retarding hay-making.
Grain has shown remarkable improvement
Fince the recent rains in all the grain districts
north of the Tehachapi and even in Southern
California the prospects are much improved.
Late grain has made rapid growth and is look
ing strong and healthy. The yield of wheat
and barley in some sections will probably
exceed that of last season. Some grain has
been harvested in the San Joaquin Valley, but
the full harvest will not commence for a week
or ten days. Haying is progressing in nearly
all &eetions and a good crop of excellent hay
is being harvested. Sugar beets, beans, hops,
corn and vegetables are in good condition.
GENERAL SUMMARY.
The weekly report of A. G." McAdie, section
director of the climate and crop service of
the Weather Bureau, is as follows:
The Weather and Crops.
Dakotas. During the past twenty-four hours
the pressure has risen rapidly over California
and has fallen rapidly over the Upper Mis
souri Valley.
The temperature has risen from 8 to 20 de
grees in the Sacramento and San Joaquin val
leys and has fallen 30 degrees or more - over
Northern Utah and Southern Idaho. In the
great valleys of California the temperatures
are still about 10 degrees below the normal.
Rain has fallen over the northern portion
of the Pacific slope and also in Utah and
Northern Nevada.

CLOSING.
A holiday feeling pervaded the stock market
here to-day. The stagnation was general and
all stocks were heavy. American shares were
hardly mentioned. - They stuck at the lower
level set by New York, with the single excep
tion of Louisville and Nashville and Union Pa
cific, which were quietly bought up. Money
rates were unaltered. -

formances closed last Sunday evening,
and through a misunderstanding over the
amount due them they created a disturb
ance. The following day William Bracket,

was. at the theater and the boys were paid

XEW TORK. May 2Lâ A Sun special
from Manila says: It is announced from
the headquarters of the Federal party
that negotiations looking to the surren
der of General Malvar have failed and
tbet General Malvar has proclaimed him
self dictator in succession to Agulnaldo.
It !? said he will reconstruct the insur
gent force of some 1800 men who are scat
lered In Batanpas, Tayabas. Laguna and
Mlndoro, and the new insurgent army
will be commanded by four generals.
The leaders of the Federal party deplore
the threatened continuation of hostilities
in the rich provinces above mentioned.
They say MaJavar'e anti-American wife
is responsible for his refusing to surren
der. It is probable a strong expedition
will be sent against him and his rendez
vous for the insurgents destroyed.
MANILLA, May 2L-E. C. Lawrence, for
merly a private in the Thirty-third Vol
unteer Infantry and afterward employed
as a civilian clerk In the adjutant gener
al's office at Manila, has been accused of
forging the signature of Captain Slavens.
the insular disbursing officer, to certain
checks purporting to be payable to Gen
eral MacArthur. On the backs of the
checks were forged indorsements of Gen
eral MacArthur to General Shatter and
to E. C. Lawrence. Two of these checks
of 5200 each have been cashed at San
Francisco, but another negotiated at St
Louis has been returned to Manila and
pronounced spurious by Captain Slavens.
Lawrence is suspected of other forgeries.
The insurgents to-day made an unsuc
cessful attack upon the village of Nacar
lan. in LAguna province. A private in the
Eighth Infantry was killed in the engage
ment. The official report of the recent en
gagement near Pasacoa, in South .Cainar
ine province, says that three American
soldiers were killed and one native scout
was captured.
General MacArthur has ordered reports
from all the civil departments preparatory
to his own final report.
The commission has prepared a bill to
create a new weather bureau. This bill
practically continues and subsidizes the
present Jesuit observatory in Manila. This
bureau has always been comDetently ad
ministered and was supported by the
Spanish Government and was later finan
cially assisted by the American military
authorities. The professor in charge of
This bureau will be appointed the new di
rector thereof at a salary of $2500 a year.
The director will make all appointments
to the auxiliary weather bureaus through
out the archipelago subject to the civil
service regulations.
